Problem: Determine the interval of convergence for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{|-2,\infty|}

Reference Answer: Alright, let's tackle this interval of convergence problem for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, okay. So, interval of convergence usually involves using the Ratio Test or the Root Test. Since this series has factorials and exponentials, maybe the Ratio Test is the way to go? Let me recall that the Ratio Test involves taking the limit of the absolute value of a_{k+1}/a_k as k approaches infinity, and then seeing if that limit is less than 1 for convergence. First, let me write out the general term of the series: \( a_k = \frac{k!(x - 2)^k}{k^k} \). So, to apply the Ratio Test, I need to compute \( L = \lim_{k \to \infty} \left| \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \right| \). Let's compute that step by step. The ratio \( \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \) would be: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \div \frac{k! (x - 2)^k}{k^k} \) Simplifying this, the division becomes multiplication by the reciprocal: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \times \frac{k^k}{k! (x - 2)^k} \) Now, let's cancel out terms. The (k+1)! divided by k! is just (k+1). Similarly, (x - 2)^{k+1} divided by (x - 2)^k is (x - 2). Then, we have k^k divided by (k+1)^{k+1}. So putting it all together: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \) Simplify the exponents in the denominator: (k+1)^{k+1} = (k+1)^k \times (k+1). Therefore, the expression becomes: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k \times (k+1)} \) The (k+1) in the numerator and denominator cancels out, leaving: \( (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k} \) Which can be written as: \( (x - 2) \times \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) Now, let's compute the limit as k approaches infinity of that expression. The term \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) is a familiar limit. Let me recall that \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Wait, that's similar to the limit definition of 1/e. Let's see: \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Let me make a substitution: let n = k + 1, so as k approaches infinity, n approaches infinity as well. Then, the expression becomes \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{n - 1} = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \times \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \). As n approaches infinity, \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \) approaches 1/e, and \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \) approaches 1. Therefore, the entire limit is 1/e. So, the limit \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \frac{1}{e} \). Therefore, the limit L from the Ratio Test is: \( L = |x - 2| \times \frac{1}{e} \) For convergence, we need L < 1: \( \frac{|x - 2|}{e} < 1 \implies |x - 2| < e \) So, the radius of convergence is e, and the interval is centered at x = 2 with radius e. So, the interval is (2 - e, 2 + e). But we need to check the endpoints, x = 2 - e and x = 2 + e, to see if the series converges there. First, let's check x = 2 + e. Substitute x = 2 + e into the original series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (e)^k}{k^k} \) Simplify the term: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, is this convergent? Perhaps we can use Stirling's approximation for k!, which is \( k! \approx k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Let's substitute that into the term: \( \frac{k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \times e^k}{k^k} = \sqrt{2\pi k} \) So, the general term behaves like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which goes to infinity as k approaches infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms is not zero; hence, the series diverges at x = 2 + e. Now, check x = 2 - e. Substitute x = 2 - e into the series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \) So, the terms are \( \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \), which is \( (-1)^k \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Similar to the previous case, using Stirling's approximation: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \approx \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Therefore, the general term is \( (-1)^k \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which alternates but the absolute value still tends to infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms does not approach zero, so by the Divergence Test, the series diverges at x = 2 - e as well. Therefore, both endpoints diverge, so the interval of convergence is entirely open: (2 - e, 2 + e). Wait a second, but let me verify once again the endpoint substitution. For x = 2 + e, we have (x - 2) = e, so the term becomes e^k. So, the term is k! e^k /k^k. Using Stirling's formula: k! ~ sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k. So, substituting: sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k * e^k /k^k = sqrt(2 pi k). So, yes, the terms go to infinity, so divergence. Similarly, at x = 2 - e, the terms are (-e)^k * k! /k^k, which alternates in sign but magnitude is the same as before, sqrt(2 pi k), so again, the terms don't approach zero. Hence, divergence at both endpoints. Therefore, the interval of convergence is indeed (2 - e, 2 + e), excluding the endpoints. But wait, just to make sure I didn't make a mistake in the Ratio Test computation. Let me go through it again. Starting with a_k = k! (x - 2)^k /k^k. Compute a_{k+1}/a_k: [(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1} / (k+1)^{k+1}] / [k! (x - 2)^k /k^k] = (k+1)(x - 2) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * (k+1) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * k^k / (k+1)^k = (x - 2) * [k/(k+1)]^k Then, the limit as k->infty [k/(k+1)]^k = 1/e. Therefore, L = |x - 2| / e. So, convergence when |x - 2| < e, so radius e, center at 2. Then, endpoints checked, both diverge. So yes, the interval is open. Therefore, the final answer is (2 - e, 2 + e). In boxed form, that's \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)}. **Final Answer** The interval of convergence is \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)}. </think> To determine the interval of convergence for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \), we use the Ratio Test. First, we consider the general term \( a_k = \frac{k!(x - 2)^k}{k^k} \). Applying the Ratio Test, we compute the limit: \[ L = \lim_{k \to \infty} \left| \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \right| \] Simplifying the ratio \( \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \): \[ \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \times \frac{k^k}{k! (x - 2)^k} = (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \] This simplifies further to: \[ (x - 2) \times \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \] Taking the limit as \( k \to \infty \), we use the known result \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \frac{1}{e} \). Thus, the limit \( L \) becomes: \[ L = |x - 2| \times \frac{1}{e} \] For convergence, we need \( L < 1 \): \[ \frac{|x - 2|}{e} < 1 \implies |x - 2| < e \] This gives the radius of convergence as \( e \), centered at \( x = 2 \). The interval is \( (2 - e, 2 + e) \). Next, we check the endpoints \( x = 2 \pm e \): - For \( x = 2 + e \), the series becomes \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Using Stirling's approximation, the terms behave like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which diverges. - For \( x = 2 - e \), the series becomes \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \). The terms alternate but their magnitude still behaves like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which also diverges. Thus, both endpoints diverge, confirming the interval of convergence is: \[ \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)} \]

Thus, both endpoints diverge, confirming the interval of convergence is: \[ \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)} \]

Problem: Determine the interval of convergence for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{(-\infty, 2)}

Reference Answer: Alright, let's tackle this interval of convergence problem for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, okay. So, interval of convergence usually involves using the Ratio Test or the Root Test. Since this series has factorials and exponentials, maybe the Ratio Test is the way to go? Let me recall that the Ratio Test involves taking the limit of the absolute value of a_{k+1}/a_k as k approaches infinity, and then seeing if that limit is less than 1 for convergence. First, let me write out the general term of the series: \( a_k = \frac{k!(x - 2)^k}{k^k} \). So, to apply the Ratio Test, I need to compute \( L = \lim_{k \to \infty} \left| \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \right| \). Let's compute that step by step. The ratio \( \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \) would be: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \div \frac{k! (x - 2)^k}{k^k} \) Simplifying this, the division becomes multiplication by the reciprocal: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \times \frac{k^k}{k! (x - 2)^k} \) Now, let's cancel out terms. The (k+1)! divided by k! is just (k+1). Similarly, (x - 2)^{k+1} divided by (x - 2)^k is (x - 2). Then, we have k^k divided by (k+1)^{k+1}. So putting it all together: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \) Simplify the exponents in the denominator: (k+1)^{k+1} = (k+1)^k \times (k+1). Therefore, the expression becomes: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k \times (k+1)} \) The (k+1) in the numerator and denominator cancels out, leaving: \( (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k} \) Which can be written as: \( (x - 2) \times \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) Now, let's compute the limit as k approaches infinity of that expression. The term \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) is a familiar limit. Let me recall that \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Wait, that's similar to the limit definition of 1/e. Let's see: \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Let me make a substitution: let n = k + 1, so as k approaches infinity, n approaches infinity as well. Then, the expression becomes \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{n - 1} = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \times \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \). As n approaches infinity, \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \) approaches 1/e, and \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \) approaches 1. Therefore, the entire limit is 1/e. So, the limit \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \frac{1}{e} \). Therefore, the limit L from the Ratio Test is: \( L = |x - 2| \times \frac{1}{e} \) For convergence, we need L < 1: \( \frac{|x - 2|}{e} < 1 \implies |x - 2| < e \) So, the radius of convergence is e, and the interval is centered at x = 2 with radius e. So, the interval is (2 - e, 2 + e). But we need to check the endpoints, x = 2 - e and x = 2 + e, to see if the series converges there. First, let's check x = 2 + e. Substitute x = 2 + e into the original series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (e)^k}{k^k} \) Simplify the term: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, is this convergent? Perhaps we can use Stirling's approximation for k!, which is \( k! \approx k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Let's substitute that into the term: \( \frac{k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \times e^k}{k^k} = \sqrt{2\pi k} \) So, the general term behaves like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which goes to infinity as k approaches infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms is not zero; hence, the series diverges at x = 2 + e. Now, check x = 2 - e. Substitute x = 2 - e into the series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \) So, the terms are \( \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \), which is \( (-1)^k \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Similar to the previous case, using Stirling's approximation: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \approx \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Therefore, the general term is \( (-1)^k \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which alternates but the absolute value still tends to infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms does not approach zero, so by the Divergence Test, the series diverges at x = 2 - e as well. Therefore, both endpoints diverge, so the interval of convergence is entirely open: (2 - e, 2 + e). Wait a second, but let me verify once again the endpoint substitution. For x = 2 + e, we have (x - 2) = e, so the term becomes e^k. So, the term is k! e^k /k^k. Using Stirling's formula: k! ~ sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k. So, substituting: sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k * e^k /k^k = sqrt(2 pi k). So, yes, the terms go to infinity, so divergence. Similarly, at x = 2 - e, the terms are (-e)^k * k! /k^k, which alternates in sign but magnitude is the same as before, sqrt(2 pi k), so again, the terms don't approach zero. Hence, divergence at both endpoints. Therefore, the interval of convergence is indeed (2 - e, 2 + e), excluding the endpoints. But wait, just to make sure I didn't make a mistake in the Ratio Test computation. Let me go through it again. Starting with a_k = k! (x - 2)^k /k^k. Compute a_{k+1}/a_k: [(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1} / (k+1)^{k+1}] / [k! (x - 2)^k /k^k] = (k+1)(x - 2) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * (k+1) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * k^k / (k+1)^k = (x - 2) * [k/(k+1)]^k Then, the limit as k->infty [k/(k+1)]^k = 1/e. Therefore, L = |x - 2| / e. So, convergence when |x - 2| < e, so radius e, center at 2. Then, endpoints checked, both diverge. So yes, the interval is open. Therefore, the final answer is (2 - e, 2 + e). Using Stirling's approximation, the terms behave like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which diverges. - For \( x = 2 - e \), the series becomes \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \). The terms alternate but their magnitude still behaves like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which also diverges. Thus, both endpoints diverge, confirming the interval of convergence is: \[ \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)} \]

Problem: Determine the interval of convergence for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{(-\infty, 2)}

Reference Answer: Alright, let's tackle this interval of convergence problem for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, okay. So, interval of convergence usually involves using the Ratio Test or the Root Test. Since this series has factorials and exponentials, maybe the Ratio Test is the way to go? Let me recall that the Ratio Test involves taking the limit of the absolute value of a_{k+1}/a_k as k approaches infinity, and then seeing if that limit is less than 1 for convergence. First, let me write out the general term of the series: \( a_k = \frac{k!(x - 2)^k}{k^k} \). So, to apply the Ratio Test, I need to compute \( L = \lim_{k \to \infty} \left| \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \right| \). Let's compute that step by step. The ratio \( \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \) would be: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \div \frac{k! (x - 2)^k}{k^k} \) Simplifying this, the division becomes multiplication by the reciprocal: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \times \frac{k^k}{k! (x - 2)^k} \) Now, let's cancel out terms. The (k+1)! divided by k! is just (k+1). Similarly, (x - 2)^{k+1} divided by (x - 2)^k is (x - 2). Then, we have k^k divided by (k+1)^{k+1}. So putting it all together: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \) Simplify the exponents in the denominator: (k+1)^{k+1} = (k+1)^k \times (k+1). Therefore, the expression becomes: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k \times (k+1)} \) The (k+1) in the numerator and denominator cancels out, leaving: \( (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k} \) Which can be written as: \( (x - 2) \times \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) Now, let's compute the limit as k approaches infinity of that expression. The term \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) is a familiar limit. Let me recall that \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Wait, that's similar to the limit definition of 1/e. Let's see: \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Let me make a substitution: let n = k + 1, so as k approaches infinity, n approaches infinity as well. Then, the expression becomes \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{n - 1} = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \times \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \). As n approaches infinity, \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \) approaches 1/e, and \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \) approaches 1. Therefore, the entire limit is 1/e. So, the limit \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \frac{1}{e} \). Therefore, the limit L from the Ratio Test is: \( L = |x - 2| \times \frac{1}{e} \) For convergence, we need L < 1: \( \frac{|x - 2|}{e} < 1 \implies |x - 2| < e \) So, the radius of convergence is e, and the interval is centered at x = 2 with radius e. So, the interval is (2 - e, 2 + e). But we need to check the endpoints, x = 2 - e and x = 2 + e, to see if the series converges there. First, let's check x = 2 + e. Substitute x = 2 + e into the original series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (e)^k}{k^k} \) Simplify the term: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, is this convergent? Perhaps we can use Stirling's approximation for k!, which is \( k! \approx k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Let's substitute that into the term: \( \frac{k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \times e^k}{k^k} = \sqrt{2\pi k} \) So, the general term behaves like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which goes to infinity as k approaches infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms is not zero; hence, the series diverges at x = 2 + e. Now, check x = 2 - e. Substitute x = 2 - e into the series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \) So, the terms are \( \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \), which is \( (-1)^k \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Similar to the previous case, using Stirling's approximation: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \approx \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Therefore, the general term is \( (-1)^k \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which alternates but the absolute value still tends to infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms does not approach zero, so by the Divergence Test, the series diverges at x = 2 - e as well. Therefore, both endpoints diverge, so the interval of convergence is entirely open: (2 - e, 2 + e). Wait a second, but let me verify once again the endpoint substitution. For x = 2 + e, we have (x - 2) = e, so the term becomes e^k. So, the term is k! e^k /k^k. Using Stirling's formula: k! ~ sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k. So, substituting: sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k * e^k /k^k = sqrt(2 pi k). So, yes, the terms go to infinity, so divergence. Similarly, at x = 2 - e, the terms are (-e)^k * k! /k^k, which alternates in sign but magnitude is the same as before, sqrt(2 pi k), so again, the terms don't approach zero. Hence, divergence at both endpoints. Therefore, the interval of convergence is indeed (2 - e, 2 + e), excluding the endpoints. But wait, just to make sure I didn't make a mistake in the Ratio Test computation. Let me go through it again. Starting with a_k = k! (x - 2)^k /k^k. Compute a_{k+1}/a_k: [(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1} / (k+1)^{k+1}] / [k! (x - 2)^k /k^k] = (k+1)(x - 2) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * (k+1) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * k^k / (k+1)^k = (x - 2) * [k/(k+1)]^k Then, the limit as k->infty [k/(k+1)]^k = 1/e. Therefore, L = |x - 2| / e. So, convergence when |x - 2| < e, so radius e, center at 2. Then, endpoints checked, both diverge. So yes, the interval is open. Therefore, the final answer is (2 - e, 2 + e). In boxed form, that's \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)}. **Final Answer** The interval of convergence is \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)}. </think> To determine the interval of convergence for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \), we use the Ratio Test. Thus, the limit \( L \) becomes: \[ L = |x - 2| \times \frac{1}{e} \] For convergence, we need \( L < 1 \): \[ \frac{|x - 2|}{e} < 1 \implies |x - 2| < e \] This gives the radius of convergence as \( e \), centered at \( x = 2 \). The interval is \( (2 - e, 2 + e) \). Next, we check the endpoints \( x = 2 \pm e \): - For \( x = 2 + e \), the series becomes \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Using Stirling's approximation, the terms behave like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which diverges. - For \( x = 2 - e \), the series becomes \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \). The terms alternate but their magnitude still behaves like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which also diverges. Thus, both endpoints diverge, confirming the interval of convergence is: \[ \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)} \]

Problem: Determine the interval of convergence for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{|-2,+\infty|}

Reference Answer: Alright, let's tackle this interval of convergence problem for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, okay. So, interval of convergence usually involves using the Ratio Test or the Root Test. Since this series has factorials and exponentials, maybe the Ratio Test is the way to go? Let me recall that the Ratio Test involves taking the limit of the absolute value of a_{k+1}/a_k as k approaches infinity, and then seeing if that limit is less than 1 for convergence. First, let me write out the general term of the series: \( a_k = \frac{k!(x - 2)^k}{k^k} \). So, to apply the Ratio Test, I need to compute \( L = \lim_{k \to \infty} \left| \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \right| \). Let's compute that step by step. The ratio \( \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \) would be: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \div \frac{k! (x - 2)^k}{k^k} \) Simplifying this, the division becomes multiplication by the reciprocal: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \times \frac{k^k}{k! (x - 2)^k} \) Now, let's cancel out terms. The (k+1)! divided by k! is just (k+1). Similarly, (x - 2)^{k+1} divided by (x - 2)^k is (x - 2). Then, we have k^k divided by (k+1)^{k+1}. So putting it all together: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \) Simplify the exponents in the denominator: (k+1)^{k+1} = (k+1)^k \times (k+1). Therefore, the expression becomes: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k \times (k+1)} \) The (k+1) in the numerator and denominator cancels out, leaving: \( (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k} \) Which can be written as: \( (x - 2) \times \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) Now, let's compute the limit as k approaches infinity of that expression. The term \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) is a familiar limit. Let me recall that \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Wait, that's similar to the limit definition of 1/e. Let's see: \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Let me make a substitution: let n = k + 1, so as k approaches infinity, n approaches infinity as well. Then, the expression becomes \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{n - 1} = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \times \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \). As n approaches infinity, \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \) approaches 1/e, and \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \) approaches 1. Therefore, the entire limit is 1/e. So, the limit \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \frac{1}{e} \). Therefore, the limit L from the Ratio Test is: \( L = |x - 2| \times \frac{1}{e} \) For convergence, we need L < 1: \( \frac{|x - 2|}{e} < 1 \implies |x - 2| < e \) So, the radius of convergence is e, and the interval is centered at x = 2 with radius e. So, the interval is (2 - e, 2 + e). But we need to check the endpoints, x = 2 - e and x = 2 + e, to see if the series converges there. First, let's check x = 2 + e. Substitute x = 2 + e into the original series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (e)^k}{k^k} \) Simplify the term: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, is this convergent? Perhaps we can use Stirling's approximation for k!, which is \( k! \approx k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Let's substitute that into the term: \( \frac{k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \times e^k}{k^k} = \sqrt{2\pi k} \) So, the general term behaves like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which goes to infinity as k approaches infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms is not zero; hence, the series diverges at x = 2 + e. Now, check x = 2 - e. Substitute x = 2 - e into the series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \) So, the terms are \( \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \), which is \( (-1)^k \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Similar to the previous case, using Stirling's approximation: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \approx \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Therefore, the general term is \( (-1)^k \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which alternates but the absolute value still tends to infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms does not approach zero, so by the Divergence Test, the series diverges at x = 2 - e as well. Therefore, both endpoints diverge, so the interval of convergence is entirely open: (2 - e, 2 + e). Wait a second, but let me verify once again the endpoint substitution. For x = 2 + e, we have (x - 2) = e, so the term becomes e^k. So, the term is k! e^k /k^k. Using Stirling's formula: k! ~ sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k. So, substituting: sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k * e^k /k^k = sqrt(2 pi k). So, yes, the terms go to infinity, so divergence. Similarly, at x = 2 - e, the terms are (-e)^k * k! /k^k, which alternates in sign but magnitude is the same as before, sqrt(2 pi k), so again, the terms don't approach zero. Hence, divergence at both endpoints. Therefore, the interval of convergence is indeed (2 - e, 2 + e), excluding the endpoints. But wait, just to make sure I didn't make a mistake in the Ratio Test computation. Let me go through it again. Starting with a_k = k! (x - 2)^k /k^k. Compute a_{k+1}/a_k: [(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1} / (k+1)^{k+1}] / [k! (x - 2)^k /k^k] = (k+1)(x - 2) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * (k+1) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * k^k / (k+1)^k = (x - 2) * [k/(k+1)]^k Then, the limit as k->infty [k/(k+1)]^k = 1/e. Therefore, L = |x - 2| / e. So, convergence when |x - 2| < e, so radius e, center at 2. Then, endpoints checked, both diverge. So yes, the interval is open. Therefore, the final answer is (2 - e, 2 + e). In boxed form, that's \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)}. **Final Answer** The interval of convergence is \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)}. </think> To determine the interval of convergence for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \), we use the Ratio Test. (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \times \frac{k^k}{k! (x - 2)^k} = (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \] This simplifies further to: \[ (x - 2) \times \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \] Taking the limit as \( k \to \infty \), we use the known result \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \frac{1}{e} \). Thus, the limit \( L \) becomes: \[ L = |x - 2| \times \frac{1}{e} \] For convergence, we need \( L < 1 \): \[ \frac{|x - 2|}{e} < 1 \implies |x - 2| < e \] This gives the radius of convergence as \( e \), centered at \( x = 2 \). The interval is \( (2 - e, 2 + e) \). Next, we check the endpoints \( x = 2 \pm e \): - For \( x = 2 + e \), the series becomes \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Using Stirling's approximation, the terms behave like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which diverges. - For \( x = 2 - e \), the series becomes \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \). The terms alternate but their magnitude still behaves like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which also diverges. Thus, both endpoints diverge, confirming the interval of convergence is: \[ \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)} \]

Problem: Determine the interval of convergence for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{ -2 < x < 2 }

Reference Answer: Alright, let's tackle this interval of convergence problem for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, okay. So, interval of convergence usually involves using the Ratio Test or the Root Test. Since this series has factorials and exponentials, maybe the Ratio Test is the way to go? Let me recall that the Ratio Test involves taking the limit of the absolute value of a_{k+1}/a_k as k approaches infinity, and then seeing if that limit is less than 1 for convergence. First, let me write out the general term of the series: \( a_k = \frac{k!(x - 2)^k}{k^k} \). So, to apply the Ratio Test, I need to compute \( L = \lim_{k \to \infty} \left| \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \right| \). Let's compute that step by step. The ratio \( \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \) would be: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \div \frac{k! (x - 2)^k}{k^k} \) Simplifying this, the division becomes multiplication by the reciprocal: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \times \frac{k^k}{k! (x - 2)^k} \) Now, let's cancel out terms. The (k+1)! divided by k! is just (k+1). Similarly, (x - 2)^{k+1} divided by (x - 2)^k is (x - 2). Then, we have k^k divided by (k+1)^{k+1}. So putting it all together: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \) Simplify the exponents in the denominator: (k+1)^{k+1} = (k+1)^k \times (k+1). Therefore, the expression becomes: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k \times (k+1)} \) The (k+1) in the numerator and denominator cancels out, leaving: \( (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k} \) Which can be written as: \( (x - 2) \times \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) Now, let's compute the limit as k approaches infinity of that expression. The term \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) is a familiar limit. Let me recall that \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Wait, that's similar to the limit definition of 1/e. Let's see: \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Let me make a substitution: let n = k + 1, so as k approaches infinity, n approaches infinity as well. Then, the expression becomes \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{n - 1} = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \times \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \). As n approaches infinity, \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \) approaches 1/e, and \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \) approaches 1. Therefore, the entire limit is 1/e. So, the limit \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \frac{1}{e} \). Therefore, the limit L from the Ratio Test is: \( L = |x - 2| \times \frac{1}{e} \) For convergence, we need L < 1: \( \frac{|x - 2|}{e} < 1 \implies |x - 2| < e \) So, the radius of convergence is e, and the interval is centered at x = 2 with radius e. So, the interval is (2 - e, 2 + e). But we need to check the endpoints, x = 2 - e and x = 2 + e, to see if the series converges there. First, let's check x = 2 + e. Substitute x = 2 + e into the original series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (e)^k}{k^k} \) Simplify the term: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, is this convergent? Perhaps we can use Stirling's approximation for k!, which is \( k! \approx k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Let's substitute that into the term: \( \frac{k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \times e^k}{k^k} = \sqrt{2\pi k} \) So, the general term behaves like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which goes to infinity as k approaches infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms is not zero; hence, the series diverges at x = 2 + e. Now, check x = 2 - e. Substitute x = 2 - e into the series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \) So, the terms are \( \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \), which is \( (-1)^k \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Similar to the previous case, using Stirling's approximation: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \approx \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Therefore, the general term is \( (-1)^k \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which alternates but the absolute value still tends to infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms does not approach zero, so by the Divergence Test, the series diverges at x = 2 - e as well. Therefore, both endpoints diverge, so the interval of convergence is entirely open: (2 - e, 2 + e). Wait a second, but let me verify once again the endpoint substitution. For x = 2 + e, we have (x - 2) = e, so the term becomes e^k. So, the term is k! e^k /k^k. Using Stirling's formula: k! ~ sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k. So, substituting: sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k * e^k /k^k = sqrt(2 pi k). So, yes, the terms go to infinity, so divergence. Similarly, at x = 2 - e, the terms are (-e)^k * k! /k^k, which alternates in sign but magnitude is the same as before, sqrt(2 pi k), so again, the terms don't approach zero. Hence, divergence at both endpoints. Therefore, the interval of convergence is indeed (2 - e, 2 + e), excluding the endpoints. But wait, just to make sure I didn't make a mistake in the Ratio Test computation. Let me go through it again. Starting with a_k = k! (x - 2)^k /k^k. Compute a_{k+1}/a_k: [(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1} / (k+1)^{k+1}] / [k! (x - 2)^k /k^k] = (k+1)(x - 2) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * (k+1) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * k^k / (k+1)^k = (x - 2) * [k/(k+1)]^k Then, the limit as k->infty [k/(k+1)]^k = 1/e. Therefore, L = |x - 2| / e. So, convergence when |x - 2| < e, so radius e, center at 2. Then, endpoints checked, both diverge. So yes, the interval is open. Therefore, the final answer is (2 - e, 2 + e). In boxed form, that's \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)}. **Final Answer** The interval of convergence is \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)}. </think> To determine the interval of convergence for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \), we use the Ratio Test. First, we consider the general term \( a_k = \frac{k!(x - 2)^k}{k^k} \). Applying the Ratio Test, we compute the limit: \[ L = \lim_{k \to \infty} \left| \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \right| \] Simplifying the ratio \( \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \): \[ \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \times \frac{k^k}{k! (x - 2)^k} = (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \] This simplifies further to: \[ (x - 2) \times \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \] Taking the limit as \( k \to \infty \), we use the known result \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \frac{1}{e} \). Thus, the limit \( L \) becomes: \[ L = |x - 2| \times \frac{1}{e} \] For convergence, we need \( L < 1 \): \[ \frac{|x - 2|}{e} < 1 \implies |x - 2| < e \] This gives the radius of convergence as \( e \), centered at \( x = 2 \). The interval is \( (2 - e, 2 + e) \). Next, we check the endpoints \( x = 2 \pm e \): - For \( x = 2 + e \), the series becomes \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Using Stirling's approximation, the terms behave like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which diverges. - For \( x = 2 - e \), the series becomes \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \). The terms alternate but their magnitude still behaves like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which also diverges. Thus, both endpoints diverge, confirming the interval of convergence is: \[ \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)} \]

Problem: Determine the interval of convergence for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{(-2, \infty)}

Reference Answer: Alright, let's tackle this interval of convergence problem for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, okay. So, interval of convergence usually involves using the Ratio Test or the Root Test. Since this series has factorials and exponentials, maybe the Ratio Test is the way to go? Let me recall that the Ratio Test involves taking the limit of the absolute value of a_{k+1}/a_k as k approaches infinity, and then seeing if that limit is less than 1 for convergence. First, let me write out the general term of the series: \( a_k = \frac{k!(x - 2)^k}{k^k} \). So, to apply the Ratio Test, I need to compute \( L = \lim_{k \to \infty} \left| \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \right| \). Let's compute that step by step. The ratio \( \frac{a_{k+1}}{a_k} \) would be: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \div \frac{k! (x - 2)^k}{k^k} \) Simplifying this, the division becomes multiplication by the reciprocal: \( \frac{(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1}}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \times \frac{k^k}{k! (x - 2)^k} \) Now, let's cancel out terms. The (k+1)! divided by k! is just (k+1). Similarly, (x - 2)^{k+1} divided by (x - 2)^k is (x - 2). Then, we have k^k divided by (k+1)^{k+1}. So putting it all together: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^{k+1}} \) Simplify the exponents in the denominator: (k+1)^{k+1} = (k+1)^k \times (k+1). Therefore, the expression becomes: \( (k+1) \times (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k \times (k+1)} \) The (k+1) in the numerator and denominator cancels out, leaving: \( (x - 2) \times \frac{k^k}{(k+1)^k} \) Which can be written as: \( (x - 2) \times \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) Now, let's compute the limit as k approaches infinity of that expression. The term \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k \) is a familiar limit. Let me recall that \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Wait, that's similar to the limit definition of 1/e. Let's see: \( \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{k+1} \right)^k \). Let me make a substitution: let n = k + 1, so as k approaches infinity, n approaches infinity as well. Then, the expression becomes \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{n - 1} = \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \times \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \). As n approaches infinity, \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^n \) approaches 1/e, and \( \left( 1 - \frac{1}{n} \right)^{-1} \) approaches 1. Therefore, the entire limit is 1/e. So, the limit \( \lim_{k \to \infty} \left( \frac{k}{k+1} \right)^k = \frac{1}{e} \). Therefore, the limit L from the Ratio Test is: \( L = |x - 2| \times \frac{1}{e} \) For convergence, we need L < 1: \( \frac{|x - 2|}{e} < 1 \implies |x - 2| < e \) So, the radius of convergence is e, and the interval is centered at x = 2 with radius e. So, the interval is (2 - e, 2 + e). But we need to check the endpoints, x = 2 - e and x = 2 + e, to see if the series converges there. First, let's check x = 2 + e. Substitute x = 2 + e into the original series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (e)^k}{k^k} \) Simplify the term: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Hmm, is this convergent? Perhaps we can use Stirling's approximation for k!, which is \( k! \approx k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Let's substitute that into the term: \( \frac{k^k e^{-k} \sqrt{2\pi k} \times e^k}{k^k} = \sqrt{2\pi k} \) So, the general term behaves like \( \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which goes to infinity as k approaches infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms is not zero; hence, the series diverges at x = 2 + e. Now, check x = 2 - e. Substitute x = 2 - e into the series: \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \) So, the terms are \( \frac{k! (-e)^k}{k^k} \), which is \( (-1)^k \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \). Similar to the previous case, using Stirling's approximation: \( \frac{k! e^k}{k^k} \approx \sqrt{2\pi k} \). Therefore, the general term is \( (-1)^k \sqrt{2\pi k} \), which alternates but the absolute value still tends to infinity. Therefore, the limit of the terms does not approach zero, so by the Divergence Test, the series diverges at x = 2 - e as well. Therefore, both endpoints diverge, so the interval of convergence is entirely open: (2 - e, 2 + e). Wait a second, but let me verify once again the endpoint substitution. For x = 2 + e, we have (x - 2) = e, so the term becomes e^k. So, the term is k! e^k /k^k. Using Stirling's formula: k! ~ sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k. So, substituting: sqrt(2 pi k) (k/e)^k * e^k /k^k = sqrt(2 pi k). So, yes, the terms go to infinity, so divergence. Similarly, at x = 2 - e, the terms are (-e)^k * k! /k^k, which alternates in sign but magnitude is the same as before, sqrt(2 pi k), so again, the terms don't approach zero. Hence, divergence at both endpoints. Therefore, the interval of convergence is indeed (2 - e, 2 + e), excluding the endpoints. But wait, just to make sure I didn't make a mistake in the Ratio Test computation. Let me go through it again. Starting with a_k = k! (x - 2)^k /k^k. Compute a_{k+1}/a_k: [(k+1)! (x - 2)^{k+1} / (k+1)^{k+1}] / [k! (x - 2)^k /k^k] = (k+1)(x - 2) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * (k+1) / (k+1)^{k+1} * k^k = (x - 2) * k^k / (k+1)^k = (x - 2) * [k/(k+1)]^k Then, the limit as k->infty [k/(k+1)]^k = 1/e. Therefore, L = |x - 2| / e. So, convergence when |x - 2| < e, so radius e, center at 2. Then, endpoints checked, both diverge. So yes, the interval is open. Therefore, the final answer is (2 - e, 2 + e). In boxed form, that's \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)}. **Final Answer** The interval of convergence is \boxed{(2 - e, 2 + e)}. </think> To determine the interval of convergence for the series \( \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{k!(x-2)^k}{k^k} \), we use the Ratio Test. Problem: Simplify $(2x - 5)(x + 7) - (x + 5)(2x - 1)$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: We expand each product separately: \begin{align*} (2x-5)(x+7) &= 2x(x) + 2x(7) -5(x) -5(7)\\ &=2x^2 +14x - 5x -35\\ &= 2x^2 +9x - 35 \end{align*}and \begin{align*} (x+5)(2x-1) &=x(2x) + x(-1) +5(2x) + 5(-1)\\ &=2x^2 -x + 10x -5\\ &=2x^2 +9x - 5. \end{align*}So, we have \begin{align*}&\ \ \ \ (2x-5)(x+7) - (x+5)(2x-1) \\&= 2x^2+9x -35 - (2x^2 +9x -5) = \boxed{-30}.\end{align*}

Problem: Simplify $(2x - 5)(x + 7) - (x + 5)(2x - 1)$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: We expand each product separately: \begin{align*} (2x-5)(x+7) &= 2x(x) + 2x(7) -5(x) -5(7)\\ &=2x^2 +14x - 5x -35\\ &= 2x^2 +9x - 35 \end{align*}and \begin{align*} (x+5)(2x-1) &=x(2x) + x(-1) +5(2x) + 5(-1)\\ &=2x^2 -x + 10x -5\\ &=2x^2 +9x - 5. \end{align*}So, we have \begin{align*}&\ \ \ \ (2x-5)(x+7) - (x+5)(2x-1) \\&= 2x^2+9x -35 - (2x^2 +9x -5) = \boxed{-30}.\end{align*}

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: A function $f$ is defined by $f(z) = (4 + i) z^2 + \alpha z + \gamma$ for all complex numbers $z$, where $\alpha$ and $\gamma$ are complex numbers and $i^2 = - 1$. Suppose that $f(1)$ and $f(i)$ are both real. What is the smallest possible value of $| \alpha | + |\gamma |$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{4}

Reference Answer: Let $\alpha = a + bi$ and $\gamma = c + di,$ where $a,$ $b,$ $c,$ and $d$ are real numbers. Then \begin{align*} f(1) &= (4 + i) + \alpha + \gamma = (a + c + 4) + (b + d + 1)i, \\ f(i) &= (4 + i)(-1) + \alpha i + \gamma = (-b + c - 4) + (a + d - 1)i. \end{align*}Since $f(1)$ and $f(i)$ are both real, $b + d + 1 = 0$ and $a + d - 1 = 0,$ so $a = -d + 1$ and $b = -d - 1.$ Then \begin{align*} |\alpha| + |\gamma| &= \sqrt{a^2 + b^2} + \sqrt{c^2 + d^2} \\ &= \sqrt{(-d + 1)^2 + (-d - 1)^2} + \sqrt{c^2 + d^2} \\ &= \sqrt{2d^2 + 2} + \sqrt{c^2 + d^2} \\ &\ge \sqrt{2}. \end{align*}Equality occurs when $a = 1,$ $b = -1,$ $c = 0,$ and $d = 0.$ Therefore, the minimum value is $\boxed{\sqrt{2}}.$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Then \begin{align*} f(1) &= (4 + i) + \alpha + \gamma = (a + c + 4) + (b + d + 1)i, \\ f(i) &= (4 + i)(-1) + \alpha i + \gamma = (-b + c - 4) + (a + d - 1)i. \end{align*}Since $f(1)$ and $f(i)$ are both real, $b + d + 1 = 0$ and $a + d - 1 = 0,$ so $a = -d + 1$ and $b = -d - 1.$ Then \begin{align*} |\alpha| + |\gamma| &= \sqrt{a^2 + b^2} + \sqrt{c^2 + d^2} \\ &= \sqrt{(-d + 1)^2 + (-d - 1)^2} + \sqrt{c^2 + d^2} \\ &= \sqrt{2d^2 + 2} + \sqrt{c^2 + d^2} \\ &\ge \sqrt{2}. \end{align*}Equality occurs when $a = 1,$ $b = -1,$ $c = 0,$ and $d = 0.$ Therefore, the minimum value is $\boxed{\sqrt{2}}.$

Problem: A function $f$ is defined by $f(z) = (4 + i) z^2 + \alpha z + \gamma$ for all complex numbers $z$, where $\alpha$ and $\gamma$ are complex numbers and $i^2 = - 1$. Suppose that $f(1)$ and $f(i)$ are both real. What is the smallest possible value of $| \alpha | + |\gamma |$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{4}

Reference Answer: Let $\alpha = a + bi$ and $\gamma = c + di,$ where $a,$ $b,$ $c,$ and $d$ are real numbers. Then \begin{align*} f(1) &= (4 + i) + \alpha + \gamma = (a + c + 4) + (b + d + 1)i, \\ f(i) &= (4 + i)(-1) + \alpha i + \gamma = (-b + c - 4) + (a + d - 1)i. \end{align*}Since $f(1)$ and $f(i)$ are both real, $b + d + 1 = 0$ and $a + d - 1 = 0,$ so $a = -d + 1$ and $b = -d - 1.$ Then \begin{align*} |\alpha| + |\gamma| &= \sqrt{a^2 + b^2} + \sqrt{c^2 + d^2} \\ &= \sqrt{(-d + 1)^2 + (-d - 1)^2} + \sqrt{c^2 + d^2} \\ &= \sqrt{2d^2 + 2} + \sqrt{c^2 + d^2} \\ &\ge \sqrt{2}. \end{align*}Equality occurs when $a = 1,$ $b = -1,$ $c = 0,$ and $d = 0.$ Therefore, the minimum value is $\boxed{\sqrt{2}}.$

This structure holds for both odd and even primes \(p\). This structure holds for both odd and even primes \(p\). This structure holds for both odd and even primes \(p\). This structure holds for both odd and even primes \(p\). This structure holds for both odd and even primes \(p\). Thus, the structure of the set \(E(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\) is the cyclic group of order \(p\), leading to the final answer: \[ \boxed{\mathbb{Z}_p} \]

Specifically, we need to compute \(\text{Ext}^1(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\). - Using the projective resolution of \(\mathbb{Z}_p\): \[ 0 \to \mathbb{Z} \xrightarrow{p} \mathbb{Z} \to \mathbb{Z}_p \to 0 \] - Applying \(\text{Hom}(-, \mathbb{Z})\) and considering the long exact sequence, we find: \[ \text{Ext}^1(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z}) \cong \mathbb{Z}_p \] - This result indicates that the equivalence classes of abelian extensions of \(\mathbb{Z}_p\) by \(\mathbb{Z}\) are classified by \(\mathbb{Z}_p\). 2. **Group Cohomology**: - For non-abelian extensions, we consider group cohomology \(H^2(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\). This structure holds for both odd and even primes \(p\). Thus, the structure of the set \(E(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\) is the cyclic group of order \(p\), leading to the final answer: \[ \boxed{\mathbb{Z}_p} \]

This structure holds for both odd and even primes \(p\). Therefore, the final answer is that the set E(ℤ_p, ℤ) is in bijection with ℤ_p, and its structure is that of the cyclic group of order p. **Final Answer** The set of equivalence classes of extensions is the cyclic group of order \( p \), so \( E(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z}) \) is isomorphic to \( \boxed{\mathbb{Z}_p} \). </think> To compute the equivalence classes of extensions of \(\mathbb{Z}_p\) by \(\mathbb{Z}\), denoted as \(E(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\), we consider both the group cohomology and homological algebra perspectives. 1. **Abelian Extensions**: - For abelian extensions, we use the Ext functor. Specifically, we need to compute \(\text{Ext}^1(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\). - Using the projective resolution of \(\mathbb{Z}_p\): \[ 0 \to \mathbb{Z} \xrightarrow{p} \mathbb{Z} \to \mathbb{Z}_p \to 0 \] - Applying \(\text{Hom}(-, \mathbb{Z})\) and considering the long exact sequence, we find: \[ \text{Ext}^1(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z}) \cong \mathbb{Z}_p \] - This result indicates that the equivalence classes of abelian extensions of \(\mathbb{Z}_p\) by \(\mathbb{Z}\) are classified by \(\mathbb{Z}_p\). 2. **Group Cohomology**: - For non-abelian extensions, we consider group cohomology \(H^2(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\). This structure holds for both odd and even primes \(p\). Thus, the structure of the set \(E(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\) is the cyclic group of order \(p\), leading to the final answer: \[ \boxed{\mathbb{Z}_p} \]

Specifically, we need to compute \(\text{Ext}^1(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\). - Using the projective resolution of \(\mathbb{Z}_p\): \[ 0 \to \mathbb{Z} \xrightarrow{p} \mathbb{Z} \to \mathbb{Z}_p \to 0 \] - Applying \(\text{Hom}(-, \mathbb{Z})\) and considering the long exact sequence, we find: \[ \text{Ext}^1(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z}) \cong \mathbb{Z}_p \] - This result indicates that the equivalence classes of abelian extensions of \(\mathbb{Z}_p\) by \(\mathbb{Z}\) are classified by \(\mathbb{Z}_p\). 2. **Group Cohomology**: - For non-abelian extensions, we consider group cohomology \(H^2(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\). However, since \(\mathbb{Z}\) is a trivial \(\mathbb{Z}_p\)-module and \(\mathbb{Z}\) is torsion-free, the cohomology group \(H^2(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\) also leads to the same result for trivial actions. - For \(p = 2\), non-trivial actions (inversion) are possible, but they do not contribute additional equivalence classes as shown by cohomology calculations. 3. **Conclusion**: - The set \(E(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\) of equivalence classes of extensions, whether considered as abelian extensions or group extensions with trivial action, is classified by \(\mathbb{Z}_p\). This structure holds for both odd and even primes \(p\). Thus, the structure of the set \(E(\mathbb{Z}_p, \mathbb{Z})\) is the cyclic group of order \(p\), leading to the final answer: \[ \boxed{\mathbb{Z}_p} \]
